  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f8d7bb00-c79f-4492-a435-02f085c6093d'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

As the world appears to self-destruct around them, they hold together to navigate the choppy waters of a decidedly ambiguous decade.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer798858257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13241994646658859879">I'm not sure why I gave this only three stars when I first read it five years ago. It was probably that final chapter, the 13000 word sentence, which felt too far removed stylistically from the rest of the novel to really work. This same final section remained jarring second time around, but with a near-perfect first 350 pages, I can't possibly give the whole book less than four stars.</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1695081896?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141033266" href="/book/show/2189578.The_Rotters_Club">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1695081896" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12662889492170736255">Telling his controversially romantic story <i>Jonathan Coe</i> is at the same time most ironic and nostalgic.<br/>When we grow up it seems to us that we live in the best of times. But reality may be quite different… And the middle of the seventies was the time of stagnation. <br/>“They sat and drank their pints. The tables in which their faces were dimly reflected were dark brown, the darkest brown, the colour of Bournville chocolate. The walls were a lighter brown, the colour of Dairy Milk. The carpet was brown</span>
  <span id="freeText12662889492170736255" style="display:none">Telling his controversially romantic story <i>Jonathan Coe</i> is at the same time most ironic and nostalgic.<br />When we grow up it seems to us that we live in the best of times. But reality may be quite different… And the middle of the seventies was the time of stagnation. <br />“They sat and drank their pints. The tables in which their faces were dimly reflected were dark brown, the darkest brown, the colour of Bournville chocolate. The walls were a lighter brown, the colour of Dairy Milk. The carpet was brown, with little hexagons of a slightly different brown, if you looked closely. The ceiling was meant to be off-white, but was in fact brown, browned by the nicotine smoke of a million unfiltered cigarettes. Most of the cars in the car park were brown, as were most of the clothes worn by the patrons. Nobody in the pub really noticed the predominance of brown, or if they did, thought it worth remarking upon. These were brown times.”<br />And even the flowery progressive rock – the main character’s favourite genre of music – was nothing but an emblem of escapism: ‘As the silence of seasons on we relive abridge sails afloat. As to call light the soul shall sing of the velvet sailors course on.’ Behind extravagance there is nothing but vacuity.<br />“You don’t understand what these people are about. At least with Enoch Powell you’ve got some thought behind it, something you can argue with. Christ, even the National Front’s got an ideology. Of sorts. But these people… It’s just an instinct with them. It’s just hatred. Hatred and violence.”<br />And periods of stagnation always are the most reactionary time.<br />But anyway the time of our youth is the best time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12662889492170736255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1666232292" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1666232292" />
    <a title="Chris_P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p"><img alt="Chris_P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558554241p2/44309339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1666232292?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris_P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris_P" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p">Chris_P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44309339-chris-p?shelf=5-star-diamonds">5-star-diamonds</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1666232292" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2460649794232705872"><blockquote>
  <i>It was the world, the world itself that was beyond his reach, this whole absurdly vast, complex, random, measureless construct, this never-ending ebb and flow of human relations, political relations, cultures, histories… How could anyone hope to master such things? It was not like music. Music always made sense. The music he heard that night was lucid, knowable, full of intelligence and humour, wistfulness and energy and hope. He would never understand the world, but he would always love this</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2460649794232705872" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>It was the world, the world itself that was beyond his reach, this whole absurdly vast, complex, random, measureless construct, this never-ending ebb and flow of human relations, political relations, cultures, histories… How could anyone hope to master such things? It was not like music. Music always made sense. The music he heard that night was lucid, knowable, full of intelligence and humour, wistfulness and energy and hope. He would never understand the world, but he would always love this music. He listened to this music, with God by his side, and knew that he had found a home.</i>
</blockquote><br />There are two sides of England as far as I'm concerned. One which I love and one which I hate. The former includes things like being pretty much the birthplace of rock music, the weather, the literature, anti-comformism, great films and TV series etc. England gave birth to Banksy for fuck's sake! The latter I'd rather not talk about so let's leave it at that. <i>The Rotters' Club</i> has pretty much the very essence of all that I love in British culture. <br /><br />Now, I'm quite emotional at the moment so I don't think I can be very objective about it but this is one of the greatest modern novels I've ever read. It has an alternative way of narrating, a whole lot of musical as well as political and cultural references, the well-known witty British humor, characters that one can't help but care about and love (more on that in a second) all of which blend together in a multi-dimensional story full of twists and turns. What I particularly loved and was amazed by, was how Coe managed to make me laugh and one or two sentences after, make the hair on my whole body stand on end. <br /><br />At first it was a bit hard to keep track of all the characters, as Coe throws in names as if the reader's already familiar with them. That involves the risk of losing the readers' interest and/or get tangled among his very creations. But, not only does that not happen, I actually found it amazingly genius how he so succesfully pulled that trick. What's more, many as they are, each character is whole and unique and although the story mainly revolves around Benjamin, we are almost equally involved in everybody's lives.<br /><br />I was going to talk about the originality and uniqueness of Coe's plot mechanisms but fuck that. Let's talk about feelings. So many and so intense! Someone once told me that one should be thankful for everything one gets. Although things may not be exactly as we want them, there is a profit in everything life might throw in one's way. OK, you got me. Although this notion is one of the book's themes, it's more that I actually needed an excuse to mention it. Teenage angst, fear in front of social instability, envy and eventually love, the most romantic, perfect and magical kind of love, are only some of the feelings Coe portrays through his characters whom we watch as they grow up and develop their personalities.<br /><br />Times like this, I wish I had the eloquence to write the review <i>The Rotters' Club</i> deserves. It's one of those books that make you feel the need to give to someone special as a gift so that they feel what you felt and thus communicate with each other in the way only a book, a song and tears can provide.<br /><br />4 stars and a wish for more like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2460649794232705872" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2787537976" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2787537976" />
    <a title="Paltia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7271273-paltia"><img alt="Paltia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545851814p2/7271273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2787537976?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paltia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paltia" href="/user/show/7271273-paltia">Paltia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2787537976" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18151586914398549235">Damn, I loved this book. Keeping one half of my mind on what I was reading and the rest on how I was feeling, thinking and believing in respect to what he had to say. This book kept me present in the moment - completely and authentically witnessing whatever was being conveyed. Few books manage to accomplish this. When those times came where my reading was interrupted I couldn’t wait to return to the story. Coe makes it so easy to pick up his character’s transmissions. There is zero interference.</span>
  <span id="freeText18151586914398549235" style="display:none">Damn, I loved this book. Keeping one half of my mind on what I was reading and the rest on how I was feeling, thinking and believing in respect to what he had to say. This book kept me present in the moment - completely and authentically witnessing whatever was being conveyed. Few books manage to accomplish this. When those times came where my reading was interrupted I couldn’t wait to return to the story. Coe makes it so easy to pick up his character’s transmissions. There is zero interference. This book is a celebration of life from the pathetic and ridiculous to the untamed and unspoiled kingdoms of bliss. There are acts of irresponsibility and self indulgence and of passion and pride. The trick is how he balances it all. He keeps you on the move emotionally speaking. Best of all I enjoyed the sensual expressions of the characters out in the world - some causing me to erupt in laughter to the point of tears and some to make me quietly smile and sigh with an oh yes expression. This is a story full of energy and vital potency. It’s wild and strong. It suggests, for me, that we not play to win but to laugh, feel, and grow. Do yourself a favor and read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18151586914398549235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3181217560" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3181217560" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3181217560?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=returned">returned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=disliked">disliked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=unions">unions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=contemp-novel">contemp-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=series">series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3181217560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13115815232974685043">I rarely give up on a book. If I am not enjoying a book, I usually slog my way through in the hope that eventually I will find something to like. Here I simply cannot go on any longer.<br/><br/>The writing style does not attract me in the least. The writing is long-winded. A topic is broached, and it takes ages until something is said. And then, rarely does that which is finally said, interest me. Or that which is said annoys me! For example, there is a long drawn out conversation among schoolkids—they ha</span>
  <span id="freeText13115815232974685043" style="display:none">I rarely give up on a book. If I am not enjoying a book, I usually slog my way through in the hope that eventually I will find something to like. Here I simply cannot go on any longer.<br /><br />The writing style does not attract me in the least. The writing is long-winded. A topic is broached, and it takes ages until something is said. And then, rarely does that which is finally said, interest me. Or that which is said annoys me! For example, there is a long drawn out conversation among schoolkids—they have zero, ZERO understanding of current events! Is this supposed to be amusing?! <br /><br />Another example, a boy is in a panic. He has forgotten his swimming shorts. He is sure his gym teacher will make him swim nude in the pool. I can understand his being freaked out, but the author extends this by giving readers imagined taunts the boy will be subjected to by his classmates. Those taunts relate to the size of his penis. Talk about juvenile. Yeah, I know kids could very well do exactly this, but I am not terribly interested in hearing these taunts. Furthermore, tell me why this kid cannot simply run home from school? If this isn’t possible, a reason why should be given. Then, this stupid episode is made even worse! The boy finds a discarded pair of swimming trunks in an unlocked locker. Now he is convinced God has put them there, just for him, and this tells him God exists. This is alluded to in the chapter's title! Are we supposed to be laughing at all this?! Well, unfortunately, I am not. <br /><br />This book is supposed to be humorous. The humor in this book is not my kind of humor.<br /><br />Nor am I interested in hearing about a married man who is physically attracted to young girls. It is the mentality, the thoughts in theses characters’ heads, that irritates me. Or bores me. This is pure drivel! <br /><br />Moreover, <b>IF</b> a physical attraction is felt by a character, I want the writing, the author’s words, to make me feel that attraction. I am instead revolted by the attraction.<br /> <br />Neither do I like that in the beginning of the book, a heap of characters are thrown at the reader. We are forced to listen to their conversations, and we do not yet have a clue to who they are.<br /><br />I do not relate to the characters. There is not a character I am curious to know more about.<br /><br />What I am saying is that I don’t like the writing and I don’t like the characters and the humor puts me off. The whole thing bores me to death. <br /><br />Nicholas Burns narrates the audiobook. He reads too quickly for my liking. I can hear the words, so the narration is OK. The narration gets two stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13115815232974685043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1515930" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1515930" />
    <a title="Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32865-taylor"><img alt="Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567607577p2/32865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1515930?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who like their novels to have a little bit of everything</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=the-power-of-love">the-power-of-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=in-a-land-far-away">in-a-land-far-away</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=in-a-time-long-ago">in-a-time-long-ago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=hidden-gems">hidden-gems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=have-reread">have-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=serial">serial</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1515930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7435715007443042678">Much to my delight, this held up very strong on the second read. Before I re-read it, I browsed through some of the reviews others had written on this site, and it made me nervous - maybe I just loved this book so much because I was young and it's about youth, so I just connected to it out of a common vim and vigor.<br/><br/>Not the case.<br/><br/>Not only did I love it the second time around, I think I liked it even more.<br/><br/>As much as I don't like to compare authors so much, I can't help but describe this as Rushdie </span>
  <span id="freeText7435715007443042678" style="display:none">Much to my delight, this held up very strong on the second read. Before I re-read it, I browsed through some of the reviews others had written on this site, and it made me nervous - maybe I just loved this book so much because I was young and it's about youth, so I just connected to it out of a common vim and vigor.<br /><br />Not the case.<br /><br />Not only did I love it the second time around, I think I liked it even more.<br /><br />As much as I don't like to compare authors so much, I can't help but describe this as Rushdie meets McEwan. It's got the scope of Rushdie: ensemble "cast," intersecting story lines, his dark humor and his attention to politics. As far as McEwan, the novel is based around singular events and the way they effect the group of people, and it's got that same sort of darkness (much like McEwan, Coe isn't afraid to let bad things happen to his characters).<br /><br />The lead in is a bit confusing, but it starts off with two youngsters in the year 2000-something talking about the history of how their parents know each other. The girl tells the story she knows - which is basically the entire book. It tells of four young men in grade school in England who are close friends: Ben Trotter, Phillip Chase, Doug Anderton &amp; Sean Harding. Ben Trotter and his family are the focus point for most of the story, but it rotates through each of the characters, as well as a few peripherals - their parents, their teachers, their significant others. It takes us through illicit affairs, politics (the IRA, socialists, unions, riots, terrorism), school rivalries, school crushes, and more.<br /><br />The short of it is that this is a coming of age story, but it's not just that: as I said, the scope of this is pretty huge, and even though character development is the driving force behind the story, there's just so much going on overall, though not enough to be distracting or confusing (he's bested Rushdie in that regard, but his scope is also a little more focused).<br /><br />Coe does a really wonderful job at developing his characters, even the ones who don't get a long time to narrate to us. Someone on this site said they didn't care about the characters, but I can't imagine that. Benjamin in particular is easy to relate to, with his obsessive crush on the most popular girl in school, yearnings to be a writer, confusion about politics and love of music.<br /><br />Coe also has a fantastic sense of humor - it's very diverse, ranging from the dark to the slapstick. Some of the most memorable moments come from it.<br /><br />The only thing that irked me on this read was the end of the "story" section. It's basically stream of consciousness from Benjamin's point of view, so there are no pauses, just one long run-on sentence. It's a little exhausting to read.<br /><br />Otherwise, I fell in love with this book all over again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7435715007443042678" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_746154173" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/746154173" />
    <a title="Eleni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18423957-eleni"><img alt="Eleni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363514617p2/18423957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/746154173?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eleni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eleni" href="/user/show/18423957-eleni">Eleni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer746154173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13743229915968436451"><i>‘Great guy wishes groovy chick to write, into Tull, Pink Floyd, 17-28.’<br/><br/>‘Wanted girl friend, any age, but 4 ft. 10 in. or under, all letters answered.’<br/><br/>“Guy, 18, cat lover, seeks London chick, into Sabbath. Only Freaks please.”<br/><br/>“Freaky Guy (20) wants crazy chick (16+) for love. Into Quo and Zep”<br/><br/>Leeds boy with scooter, looks OK, seeks girlfriend 17-21 for discos, concerts. Photo appreciated<br/><br/>[Note: the above are quotations from genuine lonely hearts advertisements in Sounds (1973)]</i><br/><br/>Why the hell had I n</span>
  <span id="freeText13743229915968436451" style="display:none"><i>‘Great guy wishes groovy chick to write, into Tull, Pink Floyd, 17-28.’<br><br>‘Wanted girl friend, any age, but 4 ft. 10 in. or under, all letters answered.’<br><br>“Guy, 18, cat lover, seeks London chick, into Sabbath. Only Freaks please.”<br><br>“Freaky Guy (20) wants crazy chick (16+) for love. Into Quo and Zep”<br><br>Leeds boy with scooter, looks OK, seeks girlfriend 17-21 for discos, concerts. Photo appreciated<br><br>[Note: the above are quotations from genuine lonely hearts advertisements in Sounds (1973)]</i><br><br>Why the hell had I not read any of Jonathan Coe’s books earlier??<br><br>It’s totally my fault of course, because friends and people with similar interests in literature to mine had already told me, ages ago, that I’d love him, and I suppose I did believe them, but I couldn't imagine he would be of the mindblowing kind, which - it seems - he absolutely is. In this book Jonathan Coe writes as if he was always destined to become an author; he’s that talented! <br><br>The Rotters’ Club is a novel about England in the 70s; under the shadow of the IRA, the miners’ strike and power cuts, socialists and far right populists, youth school rivalries within an environment of a <b>
  <i>“malign, inexorable divisiveness”</i>
</b> and teenage angst, blue and white collars and social class differences and music, music, MUSIC.. there’s A LOT of music in this book, which I can never resist anyway, but the really cool thing about Jonathan Coe – as if his being a fantastic author isn't enough already - is that he knows what he’s talking about. That was the age of punk rock and prog rock gods wannabes, eager to <b>
  <i>“push back the boundaries of the three-chord song”</i>
</b> and The Rotters’ Club nails it, like it nails any issue that it deals with really.<br><br>Because, mind you; this is a book <i>with</i> a lot of music; not <i>about</i> music. The Rotters’ Club works as a brilliant politically charged, opinionated and spot on (again; he knows his stuff) satirical commentary on the rebellious and existential 70s. <br><br>It’s also very moving, romantic, full of emotion.<br><br><b>
  <i>‘my paragon, callipygic enchantress, apogee of all that is pulchritudinous in this misbegotten, maculate world, will the truculent forces of peripeteia ever vouchsafe us the sweet euphoria of sybaritic congress?’</i>
</b><br><br>Top this all up with a FANTASTIC sense of humour too. This is one entertaining book with numerous <s>laugh out loud</s> elegantly funny moments, that get stuck in your head.<br><br>I mean, I can't stop giggling: The boys attempted to form an art-rock band, which had to have a Tolkienesque name of course. Following some quite serious and heavy brainstorming, <b>
  <i>“Minas Tirith”</i>
</b> was ditched in favour of <b>
  <i>“Gandalf’s Pikestaff”</i>
</b>, only to be ditched altogether as a project THE SAME DAY IT WAS FORMED, in favour of the punk oriented <b>
  <i>“The Maws of Doom”</i>
</b> band. Obviously, that was all put down on paper, because the band would change music progression; isn't that what raw teenage angst is all about? I’m convinced. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1387346988i/7581920.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And come on. That chat on the Cold War..<br><br><b>
  <i>‘Why is Berlin divided, anyway?’ Philip asked. ‘I've always wondered that.’<br>‘I don't know… I suppose there’s a river through the middle of it, isn't there? Like the Thames. I expect it’s the Danube or something.’<br>‘I thought it was something to do with the Cold War.’<br>‘Maybe.’<br>…….<br>‘What’s it all about, though, the Cold War? I mean, why’s it called the Cold War in the first place?’<br>‘Well,’ said Benjamin, struggling to raise some interest in this topic, ‘I expect it is very cold in Berlin, isn't it?’<br>‘But it’s all to do with America and Russia, I thought.’<br>Well it’s definitely cold in Russia. Everybody knows that.’<br>‘And why’s it called Watergate? What’s President Nixon supposed to have done?’<br>‘I don't know.’</i>
</b><br><br>To sum up, The Rotters’ Club is an extremely RICH reading experience. Jonathan Coe plays with words like it’s not even a big deal. Just when you think you finally get and enjoy the writing style and the narrative, BAM he throws at you a river of screaming poetry that is both delicate and intense and leaves you stunned basically. Ace.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13743229915968436451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1704659046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1704659046" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1704659046?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780375413834" href="/book/show/827666.The_Rotters_Club">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1704659046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8738588791006664123">The phrase that jumps to mind, critically, as I sit to compose a response to this novel is "Jack of all trades, master of none." <i>The Rotter's Club</i> does many things pretty well: smooth read, engrossing enough plot, interesting enough characters, fine evocation of time period (1970s) and place (Birmingham), political/social commentary/observation on class and race in that place and time so pivotal, in retrospect, to those of us of that generation, in forming today's horror show. I also personally </span>
  <span id="freeText8738588791006664123" style="display:none">The phrase that jumps to mind, critically, as I sit to compose a response to this novel is "Jack of all trades, master of none." <i>The Rotter's Club</i> does many things pretty well: smooth read, engrossing enough plot, interesting enough characters, fine evocation of time period (1970s) and place (Birmingham), political/social commentary/observation on class and race in that place and time so pivotal, in retrospect, to those of us of that generation, in forming today's horror show. I also personally loved the wittiness of some of the novel's games (particularly the puns of Lois and Ben's names), the frame around the story, and the re-occurring Hatfield and the North motif--a favorite band of mine!<br /><br />Unfortunately the sum of these parts fails to be greater than a general feeling of super-competentcy, but nothing, disappointingly, approaching shoutaboutability. I suppose the epic somehow lacked that single monolithic experiment, set piece, character, or super-original idea that would have put it over for me.<br /><br />Will I read the sequel? Sure, if I can find a used copy like I did of this one. I sort of wish I had a copy now as the story and characters do grow on one. I got used to it, but still not shouting about it.<br /><br /> </span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9988629735606084107" style="display:none">My second reading of The Rotters' club has made me notice so many little things I had somehow missed the first time round. This is Coe at his sharpest, unveiling inconvenient sides of England - the elitism, the social<br />Stratification, the racism, the destruction of welfare state under Thatcher, the war conducted against trade unions - the dark side of a country I have come to love so much, told by characters who stick with the reader long after the novel has been finished and put back in the shelf.       <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_2162005631'><a id="like_count_review_2162005631" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/2162005631?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">7 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F41038.The_Rotters_Club">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_2683266745" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2683266745" />
    <a title="Jonathan Norton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5888466-jonathan-norton"><img alt="Jonathan Norton" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2683266745?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Norton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Norton" href="/user/show/5888466-jonathan-norton">Jonathan Norton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140294668" href="/book/show/41039.The_Rotters_Club">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2683266745" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14675081196042618133">Jonathan Coe's a jolly nice chap, and his articles on various topics are interesting, above all his long campaign championing B.S.Johnson, which I remember him launching in The Spectator in 1991, culminating in the superb biography. So it's a shame that I have to admit I've never thought much of his own fiction, which always seems to be quite bland, cliched prose stapled together with awkward attempts at Johnsonian formal experimentalism, which only emphasise how far he's falling short of his he</span>
  <span id="freeText14675081196042618133" style="display:none">Jonathan Coe's a jolly nice chap, and his articles on various topics are interesting, above all his long campaign championing B.S.Johnson, which I remember him launching in The Spectator in 1991, culminating in the superb biography. So it's a shame that I have to admit I've never thought much of his own fiction, which always seems to be quite bland, cliched prose stapled together with awkward attempts at Johnsonian formal experimentalism, which only emphasise how far he's falling short of his hero.<br /><br />This particular tale of 70s adolescence has a bunch of unmemorable teenagers taking themselves very seriously whilst surrounded by adults who have walked out of an ITV sitcom from that era. The fragments of juvenilia are painful, the Serious Issues are dealt with in scenes that belong in a Scene drama from the same period, and I'm not even sure the references don't have a few anachronisms - was "Kalashnikov" a term that meant anything to British youth, until the advent of Rambo films 10 years later? <br /><br />I grew up in Redditch, just off the edge of the central world of this book (beyond Barnt Green on the train), my dad taught at a school in Kings Norton, but not a toffs academy like King William/Edward. One of many details of life at ordinary Birmingham comprehensives that is missing in this world: staff members were expected to drive Leyland-made cars and display them on the premises, to show their support of local industry. Yes, "Buy British" was a thing, that's how he had a terrible old Marina, until he crashed it. The management/union committees still existed in some form right up to the final demise of Rover Group, because I overheard a bunch of union guys grumbling about some idiot manager they had to deal with, on a train back home stopping at Longbridge, circa 2003.<br /><br />"Buddha of Suburbia" did the 70s thing better, not least because its leads got out of education by about the 3rd chapter. This is a club for cleverclogs who never truly wander off the conveyor belt, at least not in this instalment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14675081196042618133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2204808634" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2204808634" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2744544-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465764198p2/2744544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2204808634?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/2744544-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141008721" href="/book/show/827665.The_Rotters_Club">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2204808634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2641550836541141256">The Rotter’s Club is Jonathan Coe’s attempt at writing a coming of age tale. However this is Jonathan Coe and he manages to turn this seemingly innocent plot into a political allegory of sorts.<br/><br/>The setting is 1974 and a group of late teens are going through the usual trials and tribulations of that age group: There’s Benjamin, the erstwhile dreamer, Doug the wannabe journalist and Philip another person who dreams big. They fall in and out of love, have misadventures and walk into strange situatio</span>
  <span id="freeText2641550836541141256" style="display:none">The Rotter’s Club is Jonathan Coe’s attempt at writing a coming of age tale. However this is Jonathan Coe and he manages to turn this seemingly innocent plot into a political allegory of sorts.<br /><br />The setting is 1974 and a group of late teens are going through the usual trials and tribulations of that age group: There’s Benjamin, the erstwhile dreamer, Doug the wannabe journalist and Philip another person who dreams big. They fall in and out of love, have misadventures and walk into strange situations.<br /><br />In the background there’s workers strikes, racism and, this I thought was original, we get passages focusing on the group’s parents. In one case a parent creates a stronger link to the group through an unwarranted action but it’s better to read that yourself.<br /><br />The Rotter’s Club is a snapshot of 70’s Britain: a time where people suffered and yet also a time where the younger generation were learning to be free and fight against the shackles posed by society. A place where progressive rock was torn down by punk, which made teenagers break out of their cozy shell. Does Coe romanticise this era? In a way but he does present a gritty reality peppered by some funny moments.<br /><br />One thing which I did notice was how prescient this novel it. More than forty years later and Britain is now going to leave the European Union and the attitude towards the different cultures is changing. Obviously Coe did not know about this in 2003 but it’s funny how history repeats itself.<br /><br />Just as a note Coe’s experimental tendencies take over in the final part of the book, which consists of a 50 page long sentence. Do not skim over it as it contains important clues to the sequel, The Closed Circle. Other than that The Rotters Club is another excellent novel which manages to expand on politics but never appears heavy-handed or preachy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2641550836541141256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_168431527" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168431527" />
    <a title="Justin Evans" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2184529-justin-evans"><img alt="Justin Evans" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319658063p2/2184529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168431527?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Evans" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Evans" href="/user/show/2184529-justin-evans">Justin Evans</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2184529-justin-evans?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168431527" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2403867437340719317">This gave me almost everything I want. What do I want from a novel? I want it funny but sincere; hard-nosed but sentimental; readable but formally interesting; restrained but also balls to the wall. Ideally it'll be concerned with social events while grounding them in personal lives. <br/>RC isn't laugh out loud funny, but it's pretty funny. I felt a bit bad laughing at people who get excited at the culinary possibilities of sour cream and sometimes Coe takes too many cheap shots of the 'boy the seve</span>
  <span id="freeText2403867437340719317" style="display:none">This gave me almost everything I want. What do I want from a novel? I want it funny but sincere; hard-nosed but sentimental; readable but formally interesting; restrained but also balls to the wall. Ideally it'll be concerned with social events while grounding them in personal lives. <br />RC isn't laugh out loud funny, but it's pretty funny. I felt a bit bad laughing at people who get excited at the culinary possibilities of sour cream and sometimes Coe takes too many cheap shots of the 'boy the seventies sure produced some ugly haberdashery' type; I wish he'd spent more time on the ugliness of the decade's politics than of its drapes. It's sincere, too; anytime an author can make me feel sorry for the coming of punk because it meant the end of grandiose prog-crap is obviously doing something pretty impressive with his characters. <br />Given all of this, the flaws are pretty minor: the last chapter, which wikipedia tells me included for some short time the longest single sentence in English literature, is a bit jarring. It's impressive, and it works, but after the clarity and simplicity of the prose up to that point it's hard to see why that sentence was necessary. A bit too tour-de-forceish, to be honest. I also feel like the book was both a bit slippery and a bit too black and white. Labor unions are good, and government help for the poor is good. Agreed. But the unions in the seventies weren't exactly model unions, and the UK Labour party of the seventies wasn't ideal either; Coe's also a bit too quick to assimilate punk with Thatcherism. Finally, the framing narrative is either unnecessary (if it doesn't return in the sequel, which I haven't read) or poorly executed. Also [PLOT SPOILER!], Benjamin's de-conversion, if you can call it that, is ham-fisted and stupid. <br /><br />All of that said, it's such a relief to read a book by an author who is able to reflect critically on the art of fiction and to reach the conclusion that fiction and narrative are not only not lies, but the most important things in the world. I should add that if Coe ever wanted to write an entire library of books in the voice of Harding, he should go right ahead, and I'll dedicate the rest of my life to reading them. I just wish he'd featured more in this novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2403867437340719317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2758731293152946033" style="display:none">Very enjoyable read. In essence an evocation of the 1970s in Great Britain (the strikes, the Ira terrorism, the emerging punk, the arrival of Margaret Thatcher), especially seen through the eyes of teens, with Benjamin Trotter from Birmingham as a central character. Occasionally some less functional side tracks. The diversity of styles (diary excerpts, letters, monologues, dialogues, omniscient narrator) gives a certain dynamic, but is a bit artificial. <div id="review_3069346951" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3069346951" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106209310-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3069346951?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/106209310-andrew">Andrew</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3069346951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8655681510764461284">I could not finish this book. The characters are boring and flat. The author switches between the charecters so frequently and at such quantity it’s hard to keep up with the names and relations. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2064546195" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2064546195" />
    <a title="Jackie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7332619-jackie"><img alt="Jackie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330679996p2/7332619.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2064546195?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/7332619-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241967768" href="/book/show/22974632-the-rotters-club">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7332619-jackie?shelf=mobile-library-reading-group">mobile-library-reading-group</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2064546195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3615579962969276835">The Trotter family, Mum – Sheila, Dad – Colin children Ben going to King William’s , Lois working and Paul still at school, are the central group who see new people come and go through the years as they work, play, study and fight. Ben’s friends Philip Chase and Dougie Anderton are at the same school so depending upon their particular likes and dislikes, get involved with the music scene often quoting the NME (New Musical Express), the school magazine, the fights, the way life moves and bends th</span>
  <span id="freeText3615579962969276835" style="display:none">The Trotter family, Mum – Sheila, Dad – Colin children Ben going to King William’s , Lois working and Paul still at school, are the central group who see new people come and go through the years as they work, play, study and fight. Ben’s friends Philip Chase and Dougie Anderton are at the same school so depending upon their particular likes and dislikes, get involved with the music scene often quoting the NME (New Musical Express), the school magazine, the fights, the way life moves and bends them, the clothes, the hair, the pubs, the holiday in Holland, the difference of the approach to the German lads and the Dutch boys whose mother, a Jew, is no longer with them because of the War in Europe whilst Ben and Paul seem to have little understanding of the resonance that ordinary people felt when they were evacuated from Holland and Sweden. The terrible effect of the IRA bomb on Lois when she lost the love of her life, Malcolm, when she was so young and the impact her illness had on Ben, a teenager, as he visited her in hospital after Malcolm had died. They never seemed to be any problem with finances, none of the boys had part-time jobs or watched the telly or had family time – there was a concentration on school and how they reacted to situations with nothing else seeming to matter which is how youth is although it was interesting how Bill got rid of the Sugar Plumb Fairy and took to expanding his vocabulary.<br />An interesting way to learn about history and national issues which impact on local issues. Things that tend to get forgotten as each twenty-four hours rush by and we are all too busy to recall or remember. History is now!!<br />The novel was set in Northfield between 15th November 1973 and Election Day early May 1979 and it makes even the number 62 bus a bit of a character. The British Leyland Motor Corporation, Longbridge, employed Ben and Dougie’s fathers and indirectly employed Sam Chase the coach driver and Philip’s dad. The boys went to the fictitious King William’s Grammar School in Edgbaston after passing their 11+ and became members of the Rotter’s Club. The son of a junior manager and the son of a union rep, both at the same school. Was the class war really dying? It was so interesting to see how things expected to happen in 1973 and in reality how it actually worked out in 2003 when the novel was written. How some things have no ending - the mystery goes on forever as in the case of Miriam (Claire’s sister) – as does the IRA pub bombing – never any answers only questions. The new system of management and workers (elected representatives) on the board - what happened to that? I guess we shut the factories. Was Michael Edwards ‘hero or devil incarnate?’ Did the strike at Grunswick make any difference although it was a very good ploy to introduce the Dutch holiday photographs? Yes a novel that has left an impression, and I would like to know what happens to these characters although we know the No62 still runs along the Bristol Road.<br />The ending is totally exuberant with Ben looking so much to the future, so happy in his relationship with Cecily, wanting to laugh all the time ‘can life get any better' - so very happy, so infectious – even when Sam Chase (Doug’s father) who is reading Ulysses in the Grapevine, say to him on Election Day 1979 ‘that women will never be Prime Minister of this country’ and twenty four hours later Thatcher was in at No 10. What a year!! Steve the loser who shouldn’t be a loser and Culpepper who was a loser but got all the right grades. As Ben and Philip agree when they get their exam results ‘life stinks, doesn’t it’. That is life with all its ups and downs – always has been – always will be – same old, same old! Yes – I would recommend to a friend. A book I would like to read next is: The Closed Circle – sequel to the Rotter’s Club.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3615579962969276835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437629606" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/437629606?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tahira" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tahira" href="/user/show/2316387-tahira">Tahira</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer437629606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9414805906657232175">It took me at least 100 pages to finally settle into <u>The Rotter's Club</u>. It certainly does not fit the kind of profile of book that I tend to read, but I was feeling a little uninspired and this book was recommended to me.<br/><br/>It was hard to keep track of the layered plot lines initially, but I eventually got a hold of them. I also felt as though I would have been better equipped had I known more about Britain during the 1970s. But there was something charming about a lot of the characters, perhaps be</span>
  <span id="freeText9414805906657232175" style="display:none">It took me at least 100 pages to finally settle into <u>The Rotter's Club</u>. It certainly does not fit the kind of profile of book that I tend to read, but I was feeling a little uninspired and this book was recommended to me.<br /><br />It was hard to keep track of the layered plot lines initially, but I eventually got a hold of them. I also felt as though I would have been better equipped had I known more about Britain during the 1970s. But there was something charming about a lot of the characters, perhaps because earnest and thoughtful adolescent male protagonists have unfortunately been hard to come by, in my experience. The novel also felt very sincere, and I appreciated that quite a bit. <br /><br />I was surprised by Coe's choice in endings, though. The last narrative of the book is somewhat strangely-timed, disjointed and fairly anticlimactic considering how much ground readers have covered by the novel's conclusion. I'd be interested in seeing how this is accounted for in the sequel to <u>The Rotter's Club</u>, but I am not sure if I liked the novel enough to pursue it again.</span>

<div id="review_2330137116" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2330137116" />
    <a title="Tom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59356977-tom"><img alt="Tom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564402937p2/59356977.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2330137116?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom" href="/user/show/59356977-tom">Tom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2330137116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3052949935284271230">The Rotters’ Club is a novel set in an England in the early 1970s. A very different country to what it is now. In 1973 the UK was going through a bombing campaign by the IRA, strikes including a national miners’ strike and power cuts (an infamous 3-day week had to be employed on commerce and industry - check Youtube film of office staff having to work by candlelight), growing far left and far right populists. <br/><br/>Add to this mix school rivalries within an environment of a “malign, inexorable divisiv</span>
  <span id="freeText3052949935284271230" style="display:none">The Rotters’ Club is a novel set in an England in the early 1970s. A very different country to what it is now. In 1973 the UK was going through a bombing campaign by the IRA, strikes including a national miners’ strike and power cuts (an infamous 3-day week had to be employed on commerce and industry - check Youtube film of office staff having to work by candlelight), growing far left and far right populists. <br /><br />Add to this mix school rivalries within an environment of a “malign, inexorable divisiveness”, teenage angst, the birth of punk and the end of prog rock, blue and white collars and class and MUSIC. The 'Rotters Club' of the title is the gang/group set up by the boys in the novel. Perhaps one of best bits of this novel is the hilarious ways in which the boys try outdoing each other with ever more pretentious descriptions of the prog rock bands at the time as they delve into music journalism.<br /><br />Another 'best bit' of this novel is the love story between one of the boys' sister and her boyfriend.<br /><br />At the heart of the novel is a mystery around the disappearance of one of the characters - another reason this novel had me reading on till the end. <br /><br />I'd recommend this novel to anyone who likes books about teenage boys being teenage boys, warts and all. Or if you're nostalgic for prog rock, flares and 3-day weeks. Or the '70s.<br /><br />If I can, I'd like to recommend the TV drama made of The Rotters Club (if just to hear the Birmingham (Brummie) accent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3052949935284271230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3190793795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3190793795?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brona&#39;s Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brona&#39;s Books" href="/user/show/30281718-brona-s-books">Brona&#39;s Books</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241986479" href="/book/show/43157084-the-rotters-club">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3190793795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13774743012087569992">I'm reading Coe's trilogy about the life and times of Benjamin Trotter the wrong way round, chronologically speaking. But after reading and enjoying the third book in the series, Middle England, so much last month, I knew I had to find out how the whole thing started.<br/><br/>The Rotter's Club is a 2001 novel set in 1970's Birmingham and just like Middle England, it is a curious mix of nostalgia and satire.<br/><br/>I thoroughly enjoyed getting to know a teenage version of Ben, Philip and Doug, but I did find thei</span>
  <span id="freeText13774743012087569992" style="display:none">I'm reading Coe's trilogy about the life and times of Benjamin Trotter the wrong way round, chronologically speaking. But after reading and enjoying the third book in the series, Middle England, so much last month, I knew I had to find out how the whole thing started.<br /><br />The Rotter's Club is a 2001 novel set in 1970's Birmingham and just like Middle England, it is a curious mix of nostalgia and satire.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed getting to know a teenage version of Ben, Philip and Doug, but I did find their teenage lives a bit tedious by the end of 300 pages. Do you remember those boys at school who did nothing but talk about THEIR (superior taste in) music/politics/sport and how naff you were if you didn't agree with them? This is a book about them. I suspect Coe was one of those boys, but has come far enough through life, to be able laugh about it now!<br /><br />All this self-conscious school boy angst existed within the political and social context of IRA bombings, punk rock, immigration, union strikes and UK class consciousness.<br /><br />Coe celebrates ordinary, comfortable, middle England family life for what it was - a whole generation on the move from their working class backgrounds into more middle class privileges.<br />Full review here - <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bronasbooks.blogspot.com/2020/02/the-rotters-club-jonathan-coe.html">http://bronasbooks.blogspot.com/2020/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13774743012087569992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1887192555" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1887192555" />
    <a title="Neil Fox" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23158468-neil-fox"><img alt="Neil Fox" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528175309p2/23158468.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1887192555?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil Fox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil Fox" href="/user/show/23158468-neil-fox">Neil Fox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1887192555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18353314249650569536">In physical years, the 1970's are closer to the end of WW2 than they are to where we sit today in 2017. But as a State of mind, the 70's seem light years away, an anomaly of a Decade or a unique State of Mind that was very different to the Swinging 60's preceeding it or the 80's that came after. For myself and my contemporaries now in our late 40's, the 70's inhabit a dusty, murky corner of Childhood memory, and for friends of mine in their 50's they define an adolescence with a context that was</span>
  <span id="freeText18353314249650569536" style="display:none">In physical years, the 1970's are closer to the end of WW2 than they are to where we sit today in 2017. But as a State of mind, the 70's seem light years away, an anomaly of a Decade or a unique State of Mind that was very different to the Swinging 60's preceeding it or the 80's that came after. For myself and my contemporaries now in our late 40's, the 70's inhabit a dusty, murky corner of Childhood memory, and for friends of mine in their 50's they define an adolescence with a context that was markedly different from the 60's of their Childhood and a stark contrast to the the decade of their early adulthood that followed. The 70's are exceptionally different; in musical terms an encore to the 60's or a warm-up band for the 80's.<br /><br />Jonathan Coe's "Rotter's Club" is a trip down nostalgia lane, a look at youth, adolescence, friendship and growing up in 1970's Birmingham in the heart of Midlands England. Written from the vantage point of the turn of the new Millennium, this is wonderfully retro stuff; it's all here - the warm beer, Blue Nun, IRA bombs, brown wallpaper, militant unions, strikes, power cuts and of course the music ... and what a decade it was for music, the 70's ... from Marc Bolan &amp; T-Rex, The Who, Bowie, Status Quo, Roxy Music, Mods vrs Rockers to the coming of Punk Rock ... <br /><br />The New wave haircuts, video games, Reaganonomics and "loads a' money" City types from the 80's were a long, long way off into the future, not to mention the World of today where Newspapers are iPads, telephone boxes iPhones, Suzy Quatro has given way for Taylor Swift, Enoch Powell is Nigel Farage, warm pints have become Mojitos, the IRA has been superseded by ISIS, a nice cuppa tea is a Grande flat white and God forbid if you should ask for bacon, egg &amp; chips :- it's all Lychee shakes and JoJo berries now, if you don't mind. Birmingham City &amp; Aston Villa are also gone (for now). We can't even manage a decent strike in Britain today. In fact, the only recognizable leftover from the 70's is Jeremy Corbyn, and even then he's not much more than a tribute band to Michael Foot and Tony Benn.<br /><br />Even rooted as it is in the atmosphere of a unique decade, Coe's novel nonethless is timeless in terms of its enduring human themes and emotions, with characters that grow on you, get under your skin and have you rooting for them. The pain, anguish, heartbreak, infatuations and exhilarations of the Teenage years are central to a big, airy, heartwarming tale that rises above it's smalltown setting and elevates to something larger than itself, capturing the spirit and essence of a unique decade and evoking the timeless emotions around the coming of age.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18353314249650569536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_541838199" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/541838199" />
    <a title="Philtrum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12699702-philtrum"><img alt="Philtrum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363822007p2/12699702.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/541838199?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philtrum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philtrum" href="/user/show/12699702-philtrum">Philtrum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780754097211" href="/book/show/41042.The_Rotter_s_Club">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer541838199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8432293511142297322">It’s something of a mystery to me how I missed this book for so many years. It was published in 2001 but I didn’t get around to reading it until 2013.<br/><br/>I had been aware of it, vaguely. Had I know what it was about, I’m sure I would have read it much sooner.<br/><br/>Why? Well, aside from the fact I couldn’t have written it – not having the necessary literary skills – it might have been about my life.<br/><br/>The story concerns a group of four boys who attend a public (private) school in Birmingham (UK) in the 1970s,</span>
  <span id="freeText8432293511142297322" style="display:none">It’s something of a mystery to me how I missed this book for so many years. It was published in 2001 but I didn’t get around to reading it until 2013.<br /><br />I had been aware of it, vaguely. Had I know what it was about, I’m sure I would have read it much sooner.<br /><br />Why? Well, aside from the fact I couldn’t have written it – not having the necessary literary skills – it might have been about my life.<br /><br />The story concerns a group of four boys who attend a public (private) school in Birmingham (UK) in the 1970s, and the narrative covers 1974-1979. It’s about coming of age, class, trades unions, politics, the IRA, sex, family life, music (progressive rock mainly, but some punk), school life, interpersonal relations etc<br /><br />I get the impression the story, such as it is, is highly autobiographical. (I might be wrong). I suspect that this is why it resonates so strongly with me. I went to a public school from 1974 to 1979 (Bedford, not Birmingham). I loved prog rock (Van der Graaf Generator, Genesis, King Crimson) and punk. I had a close circle of friends at school and still look back on those days as some of the happiest (and most care-free) in my life. I remember fairly vividly the 1970s, from power-cuts in the early 70s, to the long hot summer of 76, the the Queen’s Silver Jubilee, to the general election of 1979 and the rise of the Tories.<br /><br />Wikipedia informs me Coe was born the day after me (in 1961). He has two daughters, born in 1997 and 2000. I have two daughters, born in 1997 and 2000 (though I also have three sons!).<br /><br />It’s just a marvellous tale, wonderfully told, capturing all the conflicting emotions of the times and of the lives of bright, earnest teenage lads.<br /><br />It’s not without faults. There isn’t really a proper “story”, no real arc, but this doesn’t detract from the whole for me, as, in this case, the journey is all – the destination not so important.<br /><br />The last section is a stream of consciousness chapter – all one long sentence – that, for me, was rather clumsy, and, if I’m honest, a little trite.<br /><br />All in all, for me this was a lovely, tender, funny, nostalgic book. I see there’s a sequel or sorts and I will read this in the next few weeks. <br /><br />9/10<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8432293511142297322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
